        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.8b13236"><meta property="og:title" content="Understanding the Electrochemical Compatibility and Reaction Mechanism on Na Metal and Hard Carbon Anodes of PC-Based Electrolytes for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/acsami.8b13236.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Electrolytes as an important part of sodium-ion batteries have a pivotal role for capacity, rate, and durability of electrode materials. On account of the high reduction activity of sodium metal with organic solvents, it is very important to optimize the electrolyte component to realize high stability on Na metal and hard carbon anodes. Herein, chemical and electrochemical stability of propylene carbonate (PC)-based electrolytes on sodium metal and hard carbon anodes is investigated systematically. The results demonstrate that whether using NaClO4 or NaPF6, the PC-based electrolytes are not stable on Na metal, but adding of FEC can immensely enhance the stability of the electrolyte because of the compact solid electrolyte interphase film formed. The electrolytes containing FEC also exhibit high electrochemical compatibility on hard carbon anodes, showing high reversible capacity and excellent cycling performance. A reaction mechanism based on the Na+ induction effect is proposed by spectrum and electrochemical measurements. This study can provide a new insight to optimize and develop stable PC-based electrolytes and be helpful for understanding the other electrolyte systems."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/acsami.8b13236.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da907908e9b325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da907908e9b325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '4c9ea343-43fd-4fc9-b896-a58506082a73'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da907908e9b325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Understanding the Electrochemical Compatibility and Reaction Mechanism on Na Metal and Hard Carbon Anodes of PC-Based Electrolytes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13236", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Understanding the Electrochemical Compatibility and Reaction Mechanism on Na Metal and Hard Carbon Anodes of PC-Based Electrolytes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13236", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Understanding the Electrochemical Compatibility and Reaction Mechanism on Na Metal and Hard Carbon Anodes of PC-Based Electrolytes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13236", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Understanding the Electrochemical Compatibility and Reaction Mechanism on Na Metal and Hard Carbon Anodes of PC-Based Electrolytes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13236", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Understanding the Electrochemical Compatibility and Reaction Mechanism on Na Metal and Hard Carbon Anodes of PC-Based Electrolytes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13236", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Understanding the Electrochemical Compatibility and Reaction Mechanism on Na Metal and Hard Carbon Anodes of PC-Based Electrolytes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13236", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Understanding the Electrochemical Compatibility and Reaction Mechanism on Na Metal and Hard Carbon Anodes of PC-Based Electrolytes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13236", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Understanding the Electrochemical Compatibility and Reaction Mechanism on Na Metal and Hard Carbon Anodes of PC-Based Electrolytes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13236", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Understanding the Electrochemical Compatibility and Reaction Mechanism on Na Metal and Hard Carbon Anodes of PC-Based Electrolytes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b13236", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13236r_0013.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0013.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13236&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Electrolytes as an important part of sodium-ion batteries have a pivotal role for capacity, rate, and durability of electrode materials. On account of the high reduction activity of sodium metal with organic solvents, it is very important to optimize the electrolyte component to realize high stability on Na metal and hard carbon anodes. Herein, chemical and electrochemical stability of propylene carbonate (PC)-based electrolytes on sodium metal and hard carbon anodes is investigated systematically. The results demonstrate that whether using NaClO<sub>4</sub> or NaPF<sub>6</sub>, the PC-based electrolytes are not stable on Na metal, but adding of FEC can immensely enhance the stability of the electrolyte because of the compact solid electrolyte interphase film formed. The electrolytes containing FEC also exhibit high electrochemical compatibility on hard carbon anodes, showing high reversible capacity and excellent cycling performance. A reaction mechanism based on the Na<sup>+</sup> induction effect is proposed by spectrum and electrochemical measurements. This study can provide a new insight to optimize and develop stable PC-based electrolytes and be helpful for understanding the other electrolyte systems.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.

battery</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are widely known for their large-scale energy storage because of their resource abundance and environmentally friendly nature. In recent years, more attention has been paid to SIBs, so that electrode materials have been widely developed and explored. Some cathode and anode materials have been able to meet application requirements, such as transition-metal oxides <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1â5)</a> and phosphates <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 6 to 11">(6â11)</a> for cathode materials and hard carbon <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 12 to 19">(12â19)</a> and NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 20 to 24">(20â24)</a> for anode materials. Electrolytes as an important part of the battery have a pivotal role in the electrochemical performance and practical application of SIBs. However, systematic studies on the electrolyte are overlooked. Electrolytes of SIBs mainly include the following: liquid electrolytes (nonaqueous liquid electrolytes, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 25 to 32">(25â32)</a> aqueous liquid electrolytes, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref33 ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 24 and 33 to 35">(24,33â35)</a> ionic liquids, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 36 to 42">(36â42)</a> etc.), glass and ceramic electrolytes, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48 ref49 ref50 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48 ref49 ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 43 to 51">(43â51)</a> solid polymer electrolytes, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56 ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56 ref57" aria-label="reference 52 to 57">(52â57)</a> gel polymer electrolytes, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58 ref59 ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58 ref59 ref60" aria-label="reference 58 to 60">(58â60)</a> and so forth. In these electrolytes, nonaqueous liquid electrolytes have been widely investigated for SIB systems because of their wide electrochemical window, high ionic conductivity, low viscosity, and chemical and electrochemical stability. The nonaqueous liquid electrolytes used for SIBs rely on sodium salts of NaClO<sub>4</sub>, NaPF<sub>6</sub>, NaSO<sub>3</sub>CF<sub>3</sub>, or sodium bis(trifluoromethane)sulfonamide (NaTFSI), dissolved in organic carbonate solvents, consisting of cyclic and linear carbonates or their mixtures. The cyclic carbonates, such as ethylene carbonate (EC) and propylene carbonate (PC), possess a high dielectric constant and high boiling point but low viscosity (e.g., PC) or high melting point (e.g., EC). The linear carbonates, such as dimethyl carbonate (DMC), diethyl carbonate (DEC), and ethyl methyl carbonate (EMC), have low viscosity but a low boiling point and a low dielectric constant. Therefore, general electrolytes should choose a mixture of the cyclic and linear carbonate to balance physical and chemical properties.</div><div class="NLM_p">Some of the studies have focused on the physical properties of different electrolytes, such as conductivity, viscosity, and differential scanning calorimetry, or electrochemical properties of different electrolytes on the electrode material. Ponrouch et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 25 and 26">(25,26)</a> investigated physical and chemical properties of the electrolytes with three different kinds of sodium salts (NaClO<sub>4</sub>, NaPF<sub>6</sub>, and NaTFSI) dissolved into single or mixed organic solvents, such as PC, DMC, EC/PC, EC/DEC, and so on. The results demonstrated that the hard carbon anode showed the best electrochemical properties in the electrolyte with NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in EC/PC = 1:1 solvent, which exhibited the best thermal stability in four kinds of mixed organic solvents (EC/DME, EC/DMC, EC/DEC, and EC/PC). Bhide et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> made a comparative study of different salts (NaClO<sub>4</sub>, NaPF<sub>6</sub>, and NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>) in the EC/DMC = 3:7 mixed electrolyte. The ionic conductivity of different concentrations at room temperature was recorded. The order of ionic conductivity is NaPF<sub>6</sub> &gt; NaClO<sub>4</sub> &gt; NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>. In addition, some discussions about the function and mechanism of additives <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref61" aria-label="reference 29 and 61">(29,61)</a> and other characteristics were also reported. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 30 to 32">(30â32)</a> However, few studies have attempted to systematically analyze the physicochemical properties between the electrolyte and sodium metal.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Because PC solvent has a high dielectric constant (Îµ<sub>r</sub> = 65), low melting point (â49 Â°C), high boiling point (242 Â°C), and particularly excellent electrochemical compatibility with a hard carbon anode, the PC-based electrolytes were widely used for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26 ref61 ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 25, 26, 61 and 62">(25,26,61,62)</a> To improve viscosity of PC solvent, some linear carbonates were added to ensure the good wettability of the electrode and improvement of ionic conductivity. However, systematical understanding of chemical and electrochemical stability of PC-based electrolytes on metal Na and hard carbon anodes is lacking, which would lead to conflicting measurement data and confused results. In order to obtain universal and systematical understanding on PC-based electrolytes to avoid misunderstanding of the experiments, we studied chemical and electrochemical stability of the PC-based electrolytes with different solvents, salts, and additives on metal Na and hard carbon anodes. The reaction mechanisms of PC-based electrolytes with sodium were investigated by means of <sup>13</sup>C nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR), infrared (IR) spectrum, gas chromatographyâmass spectrometry (GCâMS), ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements, and so on.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Preparation of the Electrolytes</h3><div class="NLM_p">PC and DMC, DEC, EMC (different proportions by volume) solvents with and without FEC (5% by volume) were prepared by mixing 0.8 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> (99%, Sigma-Aldrich) or NaPF<sub>6</sub> (99%, Alfa-Aesar) salt. All the solvents come from Dongguan Shanshan Co., China, 99%. The composition of each electrolyte is listed in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Tables </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a> and <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a>.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Experimental Results of Chemical Stability of Single Solvent on Na Metal<a class="ref internalNav" href="#t1fn1" aria-label="a"><sup>a</sup></a></div></div></div><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13236r_0011.gif" alt="" id="gr11"><div class="NLM_table-wrap-foot" id="IDtable-wrap-foot-t1fn1"><div class="footnote" id="t1fn1"><sup><sup>a</sup></sup><p class="last">In the table: â0â indicates no reaction; â/â means not without; and â+â means adding. The number indicates the reaction degree. From 1 to 5, the reaction degree increases gradually.</p></div></div><div></div></div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl2"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 2.
