id="IDtable-wrap-foot-t2fn1"><div class="footnote" id="t2fn1"><sup><sup>a</sup></sup><p class="last">In the table: â0â indicates no reaction; â/â means without; and â+â means adding. The number indicates the reaction degree. From 1 to 5, the reaction degree increases gradually.</p></div></div></div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Preparation of Hard Carbon Electrodes</h3><div class="NLM_p last">To prepare the electrode, 80 wt % hard carbon (HC, LBV-1001, LIB Anode Material Business Development Dept), 10 wt % carbon black (SP), and 10 wt % polyacrylic acid in water solution was milled to form a slurry, and then the slurry was pasted onto Cu foil to form the HC electrode. This electrode was vacuum-dried at 100 Â°C for 7 h. The loading amount of the active material was 1.8â2.0 mg cm<sup>â2</sup>. A CR2016 coin cell was assembled with an HC electrode as the working electrode, a Na foil as the counter electrode, and a glass fiber (GF/F, Whatman) as the separator to test the electrochemical properties.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The chargeâdischarge performance of the Na/HC and Na/Na half-cells in various electrolytes was evaluated using a battery tester (Shenzhen Neware Electronics Co., China). The coin cells were tested in a constant temperature room (25 Â°C). The impedance properties of PC-based electrolytes were performed with Autolab PGSTAT128N (Eco Chemie, Netherlands). The ionic conductivity of the electrolyte was examined by a conductivity meter (Lei-Ci DDS-307). GCâMS characterization was carried out on a Varian 450GC-320MS test station. The IR spectra were recorded on a Thermo Nicolet FTIR5700 spectrometer at room temperature. NMR spectroscopy measurements of <sup>13</sup>C were carried out at room temperature in a Bruker AVANCE III 400 MHz spectrometer. The morphological and structural features of metal Na were characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) (ZEISS MERLIN Compact). The ex situ XPS was performed by Thermo Fisher ESCALAB 250Xi.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Chemical Stability of PC-Based Electrolytes on Metal Na</h3><div class="NLM_p">Because the intercalation potential of the hard carbon anode is very close to the potential of Na metal (0 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), the chemical stability on the Na metal should be investigated first. <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Tables </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a> and <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a> summarize the reactivity between pure solvents (PC, DMC, DEC, and EMC) or mixed solvents (PC/DMC, PC/DEC, and PC/EMC in different proportions) with different salts (NaClO<sub>4</sub> or NaPF<sub>6</sub>) and Na metal. It can be seen from <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Tables </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a> and <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a> that there is no discernible reactivity between pure or mixed solvents and Na metal. However, different reaction degrees in different solvents occurred when the salts were added as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13236r_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Reaction state diagram between different NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolytes and Na metal.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13236&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl3"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 3. Reaction States of PC-Based Electrolytes with Different Additives on Na Metal</div></div></div><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13236r_0012.gif" alt="" id="gr12"><div></div></div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> shows that the compatibility between the electrolytes with different ratios and the Na metal is different when NaClO<sub>4</sub> is added. At room temperature, the mixed electrolytes showed stronger reactivity than the pure PC electrolyte with 0.8 M NaClO<sub>4</sub>. Moreover, the larger the ratios of the linear carbonate (e.g., linear carbonate/PC =&nbsp;&nbsp;3:1), the stronger is its reactivity. This indicates that adding the linear carbonate can accelerate the reaction between the mixed solvent and Na metal. For three linear carbonates, their stability ranges from high to low in the following order: DEC &gt; EMC &gt; DMC, which is in the same order with their producing free radicals. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref63'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> The results demonstrate that whether using pure PC solvent or mixed solvents, the electrolytes with NaClO<sub>4</sub> are all unstable on Na metal, indicating that the PC-based electrolytes with NaClO<sub>4</sub> cannot form a stable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film to suppress the decomposition of the solvents.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a> shows the reactivity of the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolytes with Na metal at room temperature and 60 Â°C. No obvious reaction in PC-based electrolytes with NaPF<sub>6</sub> was observed within two weeks under room temperature (25 Â°C). However, a different reactivity was detected under 60 Â°C. The pure PC electrolyte showed higher stability on Na metal than the mixed electrolytes at 60 Â°C, similar to the electrolytes with NaClO<sub>4</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>). The stability of the electrolytes with different linear carbonates range from high to low in the following order: DMC &gt; DEC &gt; EMC. This order is different from that of adding NaClO<sub>4</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>), which is possibly because of the effect of NaPF<sub>6</sub> acting as a film-formed additive. Compared to the electrolytes with NaClO<sub>4</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>), the electrolytes with NaPF<sub>6</sub> show more stablity on the Na metal, which originates from the contribution of NaPF<sub>6</sub> to form the  SEI film. However, the stability of the electrolytes with NaPF<sub>6</sub> still cannot remain under 60 Â°C, suggesting that a more stable SEI film on the electrode surface needs to be constructed.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13236r_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Reaction state diagram between different NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolytes and Na at 25 and 60 Â°C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13236&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">It is well known that the film-formed additives can be used to construct a stable SEI film to suppress the decomposition of the solvent. In order to more clearly demonstrate the effects of the additives on the stability of the electrolytes, the electrolyte of PC/DMC (1:3) + NaClO<sub>4</sub> with the strongest reactivity on Na metal was chosen to investigate the reactivity of the electrolyte after adding the additives [FEC, vinylene carbonate (VC), ethylene sulfite (ES), and lithium bis(oxalate)borate (LiBOB)]. <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">3</a> summarizes the reaction states of the electrolytes with different additives under 25 and 60 Â°C. It can be seen that adding additives can improve the compatibility of PC-based electrolytes and Na metal. However, the effects of different additives are different. The electrolytes with VC and ES just react slowly under room temperature (25 Â°C), whereas the electrolyte with LiBOB remains stable only at room temperature, but still reacts at high temperatures (60 Â°C). However, the addition of FEC can effectively inhibit the reaction of PC-based electrolytes and Na metal whether under 25 or 60 Â°C. In addition, similar experiments in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> system were also carried out, which is also in accordance with the result in the NaClO<sub>4</sub> system. In a word, FEC is more favorable in terms of forming film ability and inhibiting the side reaction than VC, ES, and LiBOB additives.</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Ionic Conductivity of Electrolytes</h3><div class="NLM_p">Ionic conductivity is an important factor for the application of electrolytes. The electrolytes with good chemical compatibility with Na metal were chosen to measure their ionic conductivities as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>. It is obviously found that the ionic conductivities of the electrolytes using NaPF<sub>6</sub> are larger than those with NaClO<sub>4</sub> in the same PC-based solvents. In addition, the effect of the addition of different linear carbonates on ionic conductivity is different. The order of the conductivity of different solvents from large to small is PC/DMC &gt; PC &gt; PC/DEC. This result implies that the addition of DMC with a high dielectric constant (Îµ<sub>r</sub> = 3.1) and low viscosity [Î· (20 Â°C) = 0.66 cP] is more effective in enhancing the ionic conductivity of the electrolyte than DEC [Îµ = 2.8 and Î· (20 Â°C) = 0.75 cP], which is governed by the concentration and mobility of charge carriers.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13236r_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Ionic conductivities of the electrolytes based on 0.8 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> and NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in various solvents (PC/linear carbonate = 1:1).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13236&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13"> Electrochemical Characteristics of PC-Based Electrolytes</h3><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) of the half-cell was performed before and after cycling in order to grasp more information about the interface film between the electrode and the electrolyte, and the electrochemical reaction characteristics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref64'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref64" aria-label="reference 64">(64)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a,b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/suppl_file/am8b13236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1</a> show the effects of different solvents (PC, PC/DMC = 1:1, and PC/DEC = 1:1), salts, and additives on EIS in Na/Na symmetrical cells (NNSCs). It is discriminated that there is one depressed semicircle in all Nyquist plots, which represents mainly the migration resistance of Na<sup>+</sup> through the SEI film. For different salts, the resistances of the cells in NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolytes are much lower (100â200 Î©, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/suppl_file/am8b13236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1a,c</a>) than those (5000â10â000 Î©, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/suppl_file/am8b13236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1b,d</a>) for the same electrolyte containing NaPF<sub>6</sub>, suggesting that the decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub> forms a low-conductive SEI film. However, it is found that the impedances in the NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte significantly increase upon time, and even reach 800â1000 Î© after 20 cycles. The impedances in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte decrease to less than 2000 Î© after 20 cycles. These results demonstrate the chemical instability between the NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte and Na metal. When 5% FEC was added, the impedances in the cell with the NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolytes increase to 1000â1500 Î©, whereas in the cell with NaPF<sub>6</sub>, electrolytes decrease to â¼2000 Î©. However, after 20 cycles, their values are almost the same (800â1000 Î©), implying that FEC can form preferentially a stable protective film on the surface of Na metal to suppress the decomposition of solvent and NaPF<sub>6</sub>.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13236r_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Impedance spectra of the NNSCs in PC-based electrolytes with 0.8 M (a) NaClO<sub>4</sub> and (b) NaPF<sub>6</sub>. Time dependence of the potential of NNSCs at 0.5 mA cm<sup>â2</sup> in different PC-based electrolytes with 0.8 M (c) NaClO<sub>4</sub> and (d) NaPF<sub>6</sub>. FEC (5% vol) is added for comparison.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13236&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The time dependence of the potential experiments was carried out to observe the polarization properties of the Na metal electrode by using the NNSCs in different PC-based electrolytes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c,d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/suppl_file/am8b13236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S2</a>). For NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolytes without FEC, the potential polarization of the cells is about 10â20 mV in initial cycles, and gradually increases with cycling (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/suppl_file/am8b13236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S2a,c</a>), suggesting that the unstable PC-based electrolytes continually react with Na metal with cycling. When adding FEC to NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolytes, it is surprising that the potential polarization of the cells becomes large (about 30â50 mV) in the initial cycle, but maintains a stable value in subsequent cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). However, when using the NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d), the cells without FEC show a very large potential polarization of &gt;100 mV, which gradually decreases with cycling, implying the formation of a low-conductive SEI film and the reconstruction of the SEI film. The presence of the NaPF<sub>6</sub>-derived SEI film also further demonstrates that NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolytes are more stable on the sodium metal than in NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolytes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>). After adding FEC, the potential polarization of the cells decreases to about 50 mV and remains stable with cycling, which is possibly because FEC forms a stable SEI film prior to NaPF<sub>6</sub>. In general, the unstable electrolyte would cause increase of the impedance and polarization of the cell during cycling, but film-forming agents (FEC and NaPF<sub>6</sub>) would maintain stable impedance characteristics. The results of the current polarization experiments are consistent with those of EIS (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a,b).</div></div><div id="sec3_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i14"> Exploration of the Reaction Mechanism</h3><div class="NLM_p">In order to study the reaction mechanism of the PC-based electrolytes with Na metal, pure PC solvent and PC solvent with different salts (0.8 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> and NaPF<sub>6</sub>) including Na metal were analyzed by using GCâMS (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>). The results show the presence of two other peaks around 4.8 and 6.4 min in the case of adding salts, besides the characteristic peaks of PC (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b,c), and it is found that the extra peaks in different salt electrolytes are the same in the electrolytes with different salts, which is indicative of the similar reaction processes of Na and PC solvent. By comparing their mass peaks, it could be deduced that the two products are diisopropyl carbonate (DIPC) and 1,1â²-oxydi-bis(2-hydroxypropyl)ether (OBHE), resulting from the decomposition of PC solvent. Also, it should be noted that no side reaction occurs in PC solvent without salts including Na metal (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a). Therefore, the occurrence of two products (DIPC and OBHE) should originate from the induction effect of the salt to accelerate the reaction of PC and Na metal.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13236r_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. GCâMS chromatograms of (a) PC + Na, (b) PC + NaPF<sub>6</sub> + Na, and (c) PC + NaClO<sub>4</sub> + Na.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13236&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further verify the reaction of the PC-based electrolytes with Na metal, IR spectroscopy was conducted to investigate reaction products in the PC electrolyte with different salts (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/suppl_file/am8b13236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3</a>). A strong band at 1100 cm<sup>â1</sup> differing from pure PC solvent was observed in PC + NaClO<sub>4</sub>, which corresponds to the characteristic peaks of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>â</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/suppl_file/am8b13236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>). Similarly, two intense peak absorptions at 850 and 550 cm<sup>â1</sup> for PC + NaPF<sub>6</sub> are associated with PF<sub>6</sub><sup>â</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/suppl_file/am8b13236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b</a>), which suggests that no reaction occurs when NaPF<sub>6</sub> or NaClO<sub>4</sub> is added to the PC solvent. Compared to the PC + NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte, the spectrum of the PC + NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte including Na metal showed different peaks mainly at about 1600 cm<sup>â1</sup>, which characterizes the COO<sup>â</sup> asymmetric stretching vibration originating from the PC decomposition on the Na metal. Similarly, the characteristic absorption peak also was observed in PC + NaPF<sub>6</sub> including Na metal (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/suppl_file/am8b13236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3c</a>), which indicates a similar side reaction as the PC + NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a).</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13236r_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) IR spectra of PC + NaClO<sub>4</sub> and PC + NaClO<sub>4</sub> including Na metal. (b) <sup>13</sup>C NMR spectra of PC including Na metal and PC + NaClO<sub>4</sub> including Na metal.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13236&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To demonstrate the reaction mechanism and the reaction product of PC with Na metal, NMR measurements were carried out as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b. For pure PC solvent, there is a one-to-one match between four resonance peaks observed and four kinds of carbon in the PC molecule. By contrast, there are other obvious resonance peaks observed except for the carbon resonance peaks of the PC molecule in the spectra of the NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte including Na metal. Two resonance peaks at about 25.7 and 68.4 ppm correspond to the CH groups and CH<sub>3</sub> groups of DIPC decomposed by the PC molecule, respectively, whereas other three resonance peaks around 23.2, 48.5, and 70.1 ppm can be assigned to the CH<sub>3</sub>, CH, and CH<sub>2</sub> position of OBHE, respectively. Likewise, for the NaPF<sub>6</sub>-added electrolyte including Na metal, these emerging resonance peaks were also observed to match the characteristics of DIPC and OBHE products (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/suppl_file/am8b13236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3d</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">On the basis of the abovementioned results, it is reasonable to conclude that the NMR results are in accordance with those of GCâMS and IR, indicating that in the presence of salt, the PC electrolyte including Na metal is unstable and easy to decompose into DIPC and OBHE products. Therefore, a cationic induction mechanism is proposed to support by the results of GCâMS, IR, and NMR (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>). The first assumption approach is that PC undergoes a ring-opening reaction via a single-electron nucleophilic attack pathway under Na<sup>+</sup> induction to produce a linear alkyl carbonate (R<sub>1</sub>COOR<sub>2</sub>) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a). The PC molecule first gets electron and is reduced as propylene and oxygen. Then, the propylene reacts with the PC molecule to produce DIPC through electrophilic addition assisted by the coordination of Na<sup>+</sup> with the carbonyl group of the PC molecule (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a). For the generation of OBHE, a possible mechanism is as follows: disodium 1,2-propanediol first generates through the nucleophilic attack of Na<sup>+</sup> and initial electronation of the carbonate molecules (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b). Then, two disodium 1,2-propanediol recombine to produce OBHE and Na<sub>2</sub>O (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b). The abovementioned reaction pathways can explain the coexistence of DIPC and OBHE when the NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte includes Na metal. Besides, it is worth noting that when adding the linear carbonate, the electrolytes become more unstable (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>), indicating that the linear carbonate accelerates the decomposition of PC. It is possibly because the linear carbonates are more likely to produce free radicals under Na<sup>+</sup> induction, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref65'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref65" aria-label="reference 29 and 65">(29,65)</a> such as CH<sub class="stack">3</sub><sup class="stack">â¢</sup> from DMC and EMC, and CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub class="stack">2</sub><sup class="stack">â¢</sup> from DEC and EMC, the free radicals can further trigger the decomposition of the PC molecule through a succession of the nucleophilic attack (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c). It can be obviously found from the abovediscussed decomposing mechanisms that the Na<sup>+</sup> induction effect has played an important role on the decomposition reaction of PC, which is the reason why the electrolyte becomes more unstable after adding salt.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13236r_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Reaction mechanism of the PC-based electrolyte system with Na metal. (a) Generation mechanism of DIPC. (b) Generation mechanism of OBHE. (c) Reaction mechanism in the presence of linear carbonates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13236&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In order to further understand the influence of the film-forming additive on the composition and morphology of the SEI film, SEM and XPS measurements for sodium tablets in different electrolytes were performed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/suppl_file/am8b13236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4âS6</a>).</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13236r_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0009.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. SEM images of different sodium tablets immersed (a) in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte and (b) in the NaClO<sub>4</sub> + FEC electrolyte. XPS spectra of the different sodium tablets immersed in the NaClO<sub>4</sub> + FEC electrolyte: (c) F 1s spectra and (d) C 1s spectra.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13236&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In the NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte, the surface of the sodium tablet is porous and has no visible SEI formation (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/suppl_file/am8b13236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). However, in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a), the surface of sodium is covered with a relatively rough layer with a large number of pores, which is ascribed to the decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub> to form a porous SEI film. For the NaClO<sub>4</sub> + FEC electrolyte (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b), some nanoparticles with a spherical or flaky shape were observed, resulting from the decomposition of FEC to form the SEI film with NaF. The compact and dense SEI can inhibit decomposition of the PC electrolyte. The decomposition products formed on the sodium tablets can be identified by XPS measurements (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>c,d, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/suppl_file/am8b13236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S5, and S6</a>). Compared to the results of sodium tablets in the NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/suppl_file/am8b13236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), the main difference is that the SEI films contain F products in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/suppl_file/am8b13236_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>) and NaClO<sub>4</sub> + FEC electrolytes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>c). The peak at 683.5 eV that is assigned to NaF in F 1s is an important component of SEI formed on the surface of sodium tablets, relating to the degradation of the NaPF<sub>6</sub> or FEC. In addition, the C 1s spectra show that the peaks corresponding to CâO, OâCâO, and NaOâCâO become more intense in the NaClO<sub>4</sub> + FEC electrolyte, which is likely due to the decomposition of the FEC on the surface of sodium tablets. These facts indicate that the addition of NaPF<sub>6</sub> or FEC in the PC-based electrolytes can produce a protective film on the surface of sodium so as to hinder the occurrence of the side reaction. Although there is no presence of the protective film in the NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte, the electrolyte would cause a continuous side reaction.</div><div class="NLM_p">According to the abovementioned discussion, the reaction schematic of the solvent and Na metal in the PC-based electrolytes is depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>. The instability of PC on Na metal mainly results from the Na<sup>+</sup> inductive effect (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a,b). The addition of the linear carbonate further promotes the decomposing reaction because of the presence of more free radicals (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>a). NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt can form the SEI film on the surface of Na metal to suppress the decomposition of PC to some extent, but the NaPF<sub>6</sub>-derived SEI film is not dense enough, leading to the continuous reaction of the decomposition of PC at 60 Â°C, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>b. It is mainly because the NaPF<sub>6</sub>-derived SEI film consists mainly of inorganic Na<sub>2</sub>O and NaF species and lacks some organic reduction products to fill the gaps among the inorganic species, resulting in the permeation of solvents to further trigger the decomposing reaction (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>b). By contrast, the FEC additive can form a compact SEI film on the surface of Na metal because of more organic reduction products combined with inorganic species, which strongly prevent the solvent from penetrating, so as to hinder more side reactions of PC with the Na metal (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>c). Additionally, the compact SEI film derived by FEC can efficiently decrease the interfacial resistance of Na metal to reduce the electrochemical polarization of Na metal and improve the electrochemical reversibility of Na metal.</div><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13236r_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0010.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. Schematic illustration of the impact on PC decomposition on the surface of the Na metal in the (a) NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte, (b) NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte, and (c) electrolyte with FEC.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13236&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i15" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i15"> Electrochemical Performance of the Hard Carbon Anode in Different Electrolytes</h3><div class="NLM_p">The abovementioned studies focus on the electrochemical compatibility of the PC-based electrolyte on Na metal. However, hard carbon is the most promising anode material for the application of SIBs. Therefore, according to the abovementioned works on the electrochemical compatibility of the PC-based electrolytes on Na metal, the electrochemical performance of hard carbon in different electrolytes was also investigated, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">10</a>. For PC electrolytes containing different film-formed additives (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">10</a>a), the hard carbon electrodes cycled in the FEC-added electrolyte exhibited outstanding behavior compared to in other electrolytes with different additives (VC, ES, and LiBOB). Besides, it is found that the hard carbon electrodes can maintain stable cyclability in other PC-based electrolytes with the FEC additive, whereas the cycling capacity dramatically decreases in the electrolytes without the FEC additive (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">10</a>bâd). In addition, the cycling performance of the hard carbon anode in the NaPF<sub>6</sub>-contained electrolyte is a little better than that in the NaClO<sub>4</sub>-contained electrolyte. The results demonstrate that the electrolyte without FEC undergoes significant decomposition on account of the instable electrolyte, indicating that the chemical and electrochemical stability of the electrolyte directly affects cycling performance of the hard carbon anode. On the basis of the results in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">10</a>, for PC-based electrolytes, 0.8 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> PC/DEC-5% FEC is an optimum choice in capacity and cycling stability of the hard carbon anode.</div><figure id="fig10" data-index="10" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 10</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/medium/am-2018-13236r_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0002.jpeg" id="gr10" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 10. (a) Cycling performance of hard carbon anodes cycled at 50 mA g<sup>â1</sup> in 0.8 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> PC-based electrolytes with different additives. (bâd) Cycling performance of hard carbon anodes cycled at 50 mA g<sup>â1</sup> in different electrolytes (PC, PCâDMC, and PCâDEC). Salt concentration is 0.8 M. PC/DEC (DMC or EMC)/FEC = 50:50:5 (v/v).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b13236/asset/images/large/am-2018-13236r_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b13236&amp;id=fig10"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i16">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">In this paper, the chemical and electrochemical compatibility of PC-based electrolytes was systematically investigated through color change of the electrolyte, EIS, and current polarization experiments. The results found that some factors including salt, additives, linear carbonates, and temperatures all affect the stability of the electrolytes. The possible mechanism was proposed based on GCâMS, IR, NMR, XPS, and SEM measurements. The following conclusions are drawn.<table class="listgroup" border="0" width="95%" id="list1" list-type="label"><tbody><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">1.</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">There is no obvious reaction observed between the pure solvent and Na metal. However, after adding salt, the electrolytes show reactive activity to some extent based on different salts and cosolvents. The film-formed additives can efficiently suppress the decomposition of the solvents. Among them, FEC is an optimum one to form the compact and dense SEI film.</p></td></tr><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">2.</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">A Na<sup>+</sup> induction mechanism was proposed to demonstrate the reaction mechanism of PC-based electrolytes with Na metal. The experiments showed that the Na<sup>+</sup> induction effect  promotes&nbsp;the formation of free radicals resulting from PC and linear carbonates to accelerate the side reaction. This mechanism can also explain well the inhibiting effect of the film-formed additive by forming a compact SEI film to isolate the solvent and Na metal.</p></td></tr><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">3.</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">Though the film-formed additives (such as NaPF<sub>6</sub> and FEC) can stabilize the electrolytes on Na metal, they increase the initial polarization and impedance of the Na metal electrode. Therefore, it is very important to find a mild additive to decrease the impedance of the electrode. This work will be a focus of our future research.</p></td></tr><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">4.</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">It is particularly important that adding the FEC additive can improve the electrochemical performance of the hard carbon anode because of the instability of PC-based electrolytes with NaClO<sub>4</sub> or NaPF<sub>6</sub> salt on Na metal. An electrolyte including 0.8 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> PC/DEC (1:1) + 5% FEC was suggested as an optimum choice to realize excellent cycling performance for the hard carbon anode.</p></td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="NLM_p last">This study only explored the electrochemical properties of the PC-based electrolytes for the Na metal and hard carbon anode. Though the optimum composition based on the PC electrolyte is helpful for the future studies of materials in the half-cell, the understanding of extension electrolyte systems (EC, ether, etc.) is still lacking. However, some proposed methods and mechanisms in this work should be referred to extend the studies on other electrolyte systems.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i17">Supporting Information</h2><div class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.8b13236" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.8b13236</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical Na-Deintercalation from NaVO2</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Didier, C.; Guignard, M.; Denage, C.; Szajwaj, O.; Ito, S.; Saadoune, I.; Darriet, J.; Delmas, C.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemical and Solid-State Letters</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">A75-A78</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">This paper describes the prepn. of NaxVO2 phases by electrochem. deintercalation.  The curves V = f(x) show that the process is reversible up to x = 0.5 and they indicate multiple single phase domains in the range 1 &lt; x &lt; 0.5.  Two single phases were successfully isolated for Na1/2VO2 and Na2/3VO2 compns.; the authors report here the 1st x-ray powder diffraction measurements of these phases.  They present a O'3-type monoclinic distortion of the O3 rhombohedral phase obsd. for Na1VO2.  The evolution of cell parameters with Na deintercalation is comparable with other AxMO2 layered oxides.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoYtKHyy3LVlrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li7LAsqKrlMKg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXivVGisrw%253D&amp;md5=f443243e7ccaf4e42e86f5a389bd437f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1039/c4ee03192j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, M. H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gonzalo, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Singh, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rojo, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Comprehensive Review of Sodium Layered Oxide: Powerful Cathode for Na-Ion Battery</span>. <i>Energy Environ.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The mechanism of NaFePO4 (de)sodiation determined by in situ X-ray diffraction</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Galceran, Montserrat; Saurel, Damien; Acebedo, Begona; Roddatis, Vladimir V.; Martin, Egoitz; Rojo, Teofilo; Casas-Cabanas, Montse</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The reaction mechanism occurring during the (de)intercalation of sodium into the host olivine FePO4 structure is thoroughly analyzed through a combination of structural and electrochem. methods.  In situ XRD expts. have confirmed that the charge and discharge reaction mechanisms are different and have revealed the existence of a solid soln. domain from 1 &lt; x &lt; 2/3 in NaxFePO4 upon charge.  The second part of the charge proceeds through a 2-phase reaction between Na2/3FePO4 and FePO4 with strongly varying soly. limits.  The strong cell mismatch between Na2/3FePO4 and FePO4 enhances the effects of the diffuse interface and therefore varying soly. limits are first obsd. here in micrometric materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrttL6foFVtlbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li7LAsqKrlMKg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXmt1Whu74%253D&amp;md5=4b0e9a529cd4c190542828ec5eff293c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1021/ja503622y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Boucher, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gaubicher, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cuisinier, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guyomard, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Moreau, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Elucidation of the Na<sub>2/3</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub> and Li<sub>2/3</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub> Intermediate Superstructure Revealing a Pseudouniform Ordering in 2D</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Fully Sodiated NaVOPO4 with Layered Structure for High-Voltage and Long-Lifespan Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fang, Yongjin; Liu, Qi; Xiao, Lifen; Rong, Yangchun; Liu, Yadong; Chen, Zhongxue; Ai, Xinping; Cao, Yuliang; Yang, Hanxi; Xie, Jian; Sun, Chengjun; Zhang, Xiaoyi; Aoun, Bachir; Xing, Xianran; Xiao, Xianghui; Ren, Yang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chem</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are the primary candidate for a low-cost and resource-abundant alternative to lithium-ion batteries for large-scale elec. storage applications.  However, the development of sodium-ion batteries is hindered by the lack of suitable cathode materials that have sufficient specific energy d. and cycle life.  Here, we report layered NaVOPO4 as a cathode material that exhibits high voltage (â¼3.5 V vs. Na/Na+), high discharge capacity (144 mAh g-1 at 0.05 C), and remarkable cyclability with 67% capacity retention over 1,000 cycles.  The excellent performances result from the high Na+ ion diffusion rate in the two-dimensional path and the reversible transformation behavior of (de)sodiation.  Particularly, this layered structure and its synthetic procedure can be extended to other alkali-metal intercalation materials, leading to other metal-ion battery systems, which opens a new avenue for large-scale energy storage systems with the development of high-energy-d. and long-life cathodes for elec. storage applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpA53vHUr_bz7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj-A1qsU7ARuQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXpsVOktLg%253D&amp;md5=3cf0e307b185dd18be1374b7e20431ab</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201502018"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ai, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hierarchical Carbon Framework Wrapped Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as a Superior High-Rate and Extended Lifespan Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv. (10-13-10-15 cm2 s-1) in the HCNP obtained at 1150 Â°C is two orders of magnitude lower than that of Li+ in graphite (10-10-10-13 cm2 s-1), indicating that reducing the carbon particle size is very important for improving electrochem. performance.  These measurements also enable a clear visualization of the stepwise reaction phases and rate changes which occur throughout the insertion/extn. processes in HCNP, The electrochem. measurements also show that the nano-sized HCNP obtained at 1150 Â°C exhibited higher practical capacity at voltages lower than 1.2 V (vs. Na/Na+), as well as a prolonged cycling stability, which is attributed to an optimum spacing of 0.366 nm between the graphitic layers and the nano particular size resulting in a low-barrier Na+ cation insertion. A summary of the current knowledge is given while emphasizing the possibility of further performance improvements by thoroughly mastering structure-property relationships and also discussing the main current bottleneck to maximize energy d. in real applications: the first cycle irreversible capacity.         </div><div class="casAbstract">As an anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), hard carbon (HC) presents high specific capacity and favorable cycling performance.  However, high cost and low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of HC seriously limit its future commercialization for SIBs.  A typical biowaste, mangosteen shell was selected as a precursor to prep. low-cost and high-performance HC via a facile one-step carbonization method, and the influence of different heat treatments on the morphologies, microstructures, and electrochem. performances was investigated systematically.  The microstructure evolution studied using X-ray diffraction, Raman, Brunauer-Emmett-Teller, and high-resoln. transmission electron microscopy, along with electrochem. measurements, reveals the optimal carbonization condition of the mangosteen shell: HC carbonized at 1500 Â°C for 2 h delivers the highest reversible capacity of â¼330 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1, a capacity retention of â¼98% after 100 cycles, and an ICE of â¼83%.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium Ion Insertion in Hollow Carbon Nanowires for Battery Applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cao, Yuliang; Xiao, Lifen; Sushko, Maria L.; Wang, Wei; Schwenzer, Birgit; Xiao, Jie; Nie, Zimin; Saraf, Laxmikant V.; Yang, Zhengguo; Liu, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hollow carbon nanowires were prepd. by pyrolysis of a hollow polyaniline nanowire precursor.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesizing Porous NaTi2(PO4)3 Nanoparticles Embedded in 3D Graphene Networks for High-Rate and Long Cycle-Life Sodium Electrodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Chao; Kopold, Peter; Ding, Yuan-Li; van Aken, Peter A.; Maier, Joachim; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6610-6618</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na ion batteries attract increasing attention for large-scale energy storage as a promising alternative to the Li counterparts in view of low cost and abundant Na source.  However, the large ion radius of Na brings about challenging thermodn. and kinetic difficulties to the electrodes for Na-storage, including low reversible capacity and low ion transport, as well as large vol. change.  To mitigate or even overcome the kinetic problems, the authors develop a self-assembly route to a novel architecture consisting of nanosized porous NASICON-type NaTi2(PO4)3 particles embedded in microsized 3-dimensional graphene network.  Such architecture synergistically combines the advantages of a 3-dimensional graphene network and of 0-dimensional porous nanoparticles.  It greatly increases the electron/ion transport kinetics and assures the electrode structure integrity, leading to attractive electrochem. performance as reflected by a high rate-capability (112 mAh g-1 at 1C, 105 mAh g-1 at 5C, 96 mAh g-1 at 10C, 67 mAh g-1 at 50C), a long cycle-life (capacity retention of 80% after 1000 cycles at 10C), and a high initial Coulombic efficiency (&gt;79%).  This nanostructure design provides a promising pathway for developing high performance NASICON-type materials for Na storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpkwqPZr8xUU7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhLRtWE5jDb_A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtVaks7%252FF&amp;md5=20c6e4cf576ecb4fcfba60e930db6bc3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200598"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Young, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Carter, W.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Grid-scale energy storage systems with low-cost and high-performance electrodes are needed to meet the requirements of sustainable energy systems.  Due to the wide abundance and low cost of sodium resources and their similar electrochem. to the established lithium-ion batteries, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted considerable interest as ideal candidates for grid-scale energy storage systems.  In the past decade, though tremendous efforts have been made to promote the development of SIBs, and significant advances have been achieved, further improvements are still required in terms of energy/power d. and long cyclic stability for commercialization.  In this review, the latest progress in electrode materials for SIBs, including a variety of promising cathodes and anodes, is briefly summarized.  Besides, the sodium storage mechanisms, endeavors on electrochem. property enhancements, structural and compositional optimizations, challenges and perspectives of the electrode materials for SIBs are discussed.  Though enormous challenges may lie ahead, we believe that through intensive research efforts, sodium-ion batteries with low operation cost and longevity will be commercialized for large-scale energy storage application in the near future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoo58FwhtNvWbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhYAspI3Uptzw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXitVaksbvK&amp;md5=1ef8733737aa6ee4f33eb1d4240e0c64</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2013.03.013"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ai, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Low-Cost and Environmentally Benign Aqueous Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Battery Based on NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>âNa<sub>2</sub>NiFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> Intercalation Chemistry</span>. <i>Electrochem.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In search of an optimized electrolyte for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ponrouch, Alexandre; Marchante, Elena; Courty, Matthieu; Tarascon, Jean-Marie; Palacin, M. Rosa</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrolytes are essential for the proper functioning of any battery technol. and the emerging Na-ion technol. is no exception.  Hence, a major focus on battery research is to identify the most appropriate formulation so as to minimize interface reactions and enhance both cell performances and safety aspects.  In order to identify suitable electrolyte formulations for Na-ion chem. we benchmarked various electrolytes contg. diverse solvent mixts. (cyclic, acyclic carbonates, glymes) and Na-based salts having either F-based or perchlorate anions and measured viscosity, ionic cond., and thermal and electrochem. stability.  The binary EC:PC solvent mixt. has emerged as the best solvent formulation and has been used to test the performance of Na/hard carbon cells with both NaClO4 and NaPF6 as dissolved salts.  Hard carbon electrodes having reversible capacities of 200 mA h g-1 with decent rate capability and excellent capacity retention (&gt;180 cycles) were demonstrated.  Moreover, DSC heating curves demonstrated that fully sodiated hard carbon cycled in NaPF6-EC:PC exhibits the highest exothermic onset temp. and nearly the lowest enthalpy of reaction, thus making this electrolyte most attractive for the development of Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp6iY9--X3CQLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhYAspI3Uptzw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38Xht1WrsLnE&amp;md5=4cc627e9703d3883d10ee8eccc42e514</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1039/c4ta04428b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ponrouch, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Monti, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Boschin, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Steen, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Johansson, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">PalacÃ­n, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The 1st review of the various electrolytes currently used and developed for Na-ion batteries (SIBs), both in terms of materials and concepts, is presented.  In contrast to the Li-ion battery (LIB), which is a mature technol. for which a more or less unanimously accepted std. electrolyte exists: 1 M LiPF6 in EC/DMC, the electrolyte of choice for SIBs has not yet fully conformed to a std.  This is true for both materials: salts, solvents, or additives, and concept, using the main track of org. solvents or aiming for other concepts.  SIB research currently prospers, benefitting from using know-how gained from 30 years of LIB R and D.  Here the currently employed electrolytes are emphasized and their effects on practical SIB performance are outlined, scrutinizing the rationale for specific choices made, salts, solvents, additives, concns., etc. for each specific cell set-up and usage conditions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpb_d9E3Qe0wLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhYAspI3Uptzw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhslOjtLfI&amp;md5=b99d9ac3e00e101e25ffc33ee1bef953</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1039/c3cp53077a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bhide, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hofmann, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Katharina

                        cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In Silico-Based Rank-Order Determination and Experiments on Nonaqueous Electrolytes for Sodium Ion Battery Applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kamath, Ganesh; Cutler, Richard W.; Deshmukh, Sanket A.; Shakourian-Fard, Mehdi; Parrish, Riley; Huether, Joshua; Butt, Darryl P.; Xiong, H.; Sankaranarayanan, Subramanian K. R. S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">118</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">25</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">13406-13416</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrolytes are an important component of electrochem. energy storage systems and their optimization is crit. for emerging beyond lithium ion technologies.  Here, an integrated computational-exptl. approach is used to rank-order and aid the selection of suitable electrolytes for a Na-ion battery.  We present an in silico strategy based on both thermodn. and kinetic descriptors derived from mol. dynamics simulations to rationally arrive at optimal electrolytes for Na-ion batteries.  We benchmarked various electrolytes (pure and binary mixts. of cyclic and acyclic carbonates with NaClO4 salt) to identify appropriate formulations with the overarching goal of simultaneously enhancing cell performance while meeting safety norms.  Fundamental insights from computationally derived thermodn. and kinetic data considerations coupled with atomistic-level description of the solvation dynamics is used to rank order the various electrolytes.  Thermodn. considerations based on free energy evaluation indicate EC:PC as a top electrolyte formulation under equil. conditions.  However, kinetic descriptors which are important factors dictating the rate capability and power performance suggest EC:DMC and EC:EMC to be among the best formulations.  Exptl. verification of these optimized formulations was carried out by examg. the electrochem. performance of various electrolytes in Na/TiO2 nanotubes half cells with NaClO4 salt.  Our rate capability studies confirm that EC:DMC and EC:EMC to be the best formulations.  These optimized formulations have low-rate specific capacities â¼120-140 mAh/g whereas the lower ranked electrolytes (EC: DEC) have capacities â¼95 mAh/g.  The various electrolytes are also evaluated from a safety perspective.  Such results suggest encouraging prospects for this approach in the a priori prediction of optimal sodium ion systems with possible screening implications for novel battery formulations.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrJZiypkQQpaLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhYAspI3Uptzw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXos1Wrt7Y%253D&amp;md5=22429c441999aebbb1dd0253e5e07e43</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2016.04.070"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Choi, N.-S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Highly Stable Linear Carbonate-Containing Electrolytes with Fluoroethylene Carbonate for High-Performance Cathodes in Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J. The pos. ion-specific images established by TOF-SIMS anal. show the nonhomogeneous distribution of various fragments from the pristine GDHC, which is caused by slightly inhomogeneous mixt. of GDHC and conducting C black (Super P) particles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqDP3tjLvxiO7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgknPd3yBK3wA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhsFGjsrrJ&amp;md5=cf95b5026bf8c439be3272311abd2b40</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2010.01.020"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Whitacre, J.                         cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na4Mn9O18 as a positive electrode material for an aqueous electrolyte sodium-ion energy storage device</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Whitacre, J.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Here we demonstrate Na4Mn9O18 as a sodium intercalation pos. electrode material for an aq. electrolyte energy storage device. Optimized Na4Mn9O18 was documented as having a specific capacity of 45 mAh/g through a voltage range of 0.5 V, or an equiv. specific capacitance of over 300 F/g.         </div><div class="casAbstract">As electrolytes for sodium secondary batteries operating over a wide temp. range, Na[FSA]-[C3C1pyrr][FSA] (FSA = bis(fluorosulfonyl)amide, C3C1pyrr = N-methyl-N-propylpyrrolidinium) ionic liqs. have been investigated.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">34</span>
